    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="0dcb2c9194294ab8990390a5b4539942">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'f1c237f4-744e-4e46-9eaf-fce14d82a329'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group  metis-abstract">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en">
         <h2 id="d23707545" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p>A hybrid ester-ether electrolyte is proposed by introducing weakly solvated tetrahydrofuran into commonly used propylene carbonate-based electrolyte, which simultaneously achieves the anion-reinforced solvation structure and high ionic conductivity. These merit the gradient inorganic-rich electrode-electrolyte interface with accelerated desolvation process, guaranteeing the fast-charging performance of Prussian blue||hard carbon full cells with high electrode mass loading.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/25329201-5fa5-4158-b5bf-d49d665f04a8/anie202410494-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/25329201-5fa5-4158-b5bf-d49d665f04a8/anie202410494-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/87e105c4-7875-47c6-a167-4e1baf37d839/anie202410494-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/25329201-5fa5-4158-b5bf-d49d665f04a8/anie202410494-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d23707743" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Anion-reinforced solvation structure favors the formation of inorganic-rich robust electrode-electrolyte interface, which endows fast ion transport and high strength modulus to enable improved electrochemical performance. However, such a unique solvation structure inevitably injures the ionic conductivity of electrolytes and limits the fast-charging performance. Herein, a trade-off in tuning anion-reinforced solvation structure and high ionic conductivity is realized by the entropy-assisted hybrid ester-ether electrolyte. Anion-reinforced solvation sheath with more anions occupying the inner Na<sup>+</sup> shell is constructed by introducing the weakly coordinated ether tetrahydrofuran into the commonly used ester-based electrolyte, which merits the accelerated desolvation energy and gradient inorganic-rich electrode-electrolyte interface. The improved ionic conductivity is attributed to the weakly diverse solvation structures induced by entropy effect. These enable the enhanced rate performance and cycling stability of Prussian blue||hard carbon full cells with high electrode mass loading. More importantly, the practical application of the designed electrolyte was further demonstrated by industry-level 18650 cylindrical cells.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202410494-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202410494-sec-0001-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Rechargeable sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have attracted significant attention in next-generation large-scale energy storage systems, because of their abundant reserves and low redox potential (−2.71 V vs. standard hydrogen electrode).<span><sup><a href="#anie202410494-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410494-bib-0001_R_d23707355e629" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1">1</a></sup></span> Compared with lithium-ion batteries, the large ionic radius and heavy atomic weight of Na<sup>+</sup> induce huge volume change and sluggish electrochemical reaction kinetics of electrode materials, which inevitably impede the long-cycle-life and fast-charging performance of SIBs.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410494-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410494-bib-0007_R_d23707355e634" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2">2</a></sup></span> Meanwhile, the repeated substantial volume changes pose greater challenges for constructing robust electrode-electrolyte interfaces (EEI) to enable superior electrochemical performance.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410494-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410494-bib-0011_R_d23707355e637" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3">3</a></sup></span> Until now, significant progress has been made in electrode materials, effectively enhancing the fast-charging performance and cycling stability of SIBs.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410494-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410494-bib-0014_R_d23707355e640" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4">4</a></sup></span> However, the investigation of the effect of EEI on the electrochemical performance of sodium-ion full batteries is still in infancy. Anion-induced robust EEI generally endows the fast ion transport and high strength modulus, which has been demonstrated as an effective strategy to boost electrochemical performance.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410494-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410494-bib-0012_R_d23707355e644" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3a - Adv. Sci.">3a</a>, <a href="#anie202410494-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410494-bib-0019_R_d23707355e644" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5">5</a></sup></span> Therefore, the exploration of electrolytes with anion-reinforced solvation sheath is of vital importance for advanced sodium-ion full batteries.</p>
         
         <p>The construction of anion-reinforced solvation sheath mainly focuses on regulating the anion proportion or adjusting the coordination ability between anion and solvents, including the following three types: (1) Increasing the proportion of anions, for example, the high-concentration electrolyte and localized high concentration electrolyte, where anions occupy the inner solvation sheath due to the insufficient coordinated solvents.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410494-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410494-bib-0022_R_d23707355e650" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6">6</a></sup></span> (2) Weakening the interaction of Na<sup>+</sup>-solvents, which can be realized by introducing weakly coordinated solvents, such as tetrahydrofuran (THF), 1,4-dioxane (1,4-DX), highly fluoridated cosolvents and so on.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410494-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410494-bib-0027_R_d23707355e655" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7">7</a></sup></span> (3) Enhancing the interaction of Na<sup>+</sup>-anions, by employing the strongly coordinated anions of nitrate (NO<sub>3</sub><sup>−</sup>), trifluoroacetate (TFA<sup>−</sup>), etc.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410494-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410494-bib-0033_R_d23707355e666" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8">8</a></sup></span> However, these anion-reinforced electrolytes endow the low dissociation degree of sodium salt with low ionic conductivity, which limits their improvement of interfacial kinetics. In addition, the application in practical sodium-ion full batteries with high electrode mass loading remains extremely rare. Reasonable electrolyte design of anion-reinforced solvation sheath with high ionic conductivity is yet challenging but worth further exploring for practical fast-charging sodium-ion full batteries.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, a hybrid ether-ester electrolyte with anion-reinforced solvation structure and high ionic conductivity is proposed, which induces the formation of anion-derived robust electrode-electrolyte interface. Weakly solvated ether-based solvent, tetrahydrofuran THF, is introduced into propylene carbonate (PC)-based electrolyte as 1.0 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>-PC/THF, regulating more PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anions to participate in the inner Na<sup>+</sup> sheath and accelerating the desolvation process. Moreover, the weakly solvating structures with increased molecular diversity induced by entropy effect merit enhanced ionic conductivity with fast interfacial dynamics. These enable the stable long-term cycling of 18650 cylindrical Prussian blue||hard carbon (PB||HC) full cell with capacity retention of 82.5 % after 100 cycles. This work highlights the potential of entropy-assisted hybrid ether-ester electrolytes for advanced sodium-ion batteries.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202410494-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202410494-sec-0002-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202410494-sec-0003">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202410494-sec-0003-title">2.1 Electrolyte Design</h3>
            
            <p>Commonly used ester-based electrolytes mainly belong to the strongly solvated electrolyte, which endow high conductivity but at the cost of solvent-derived EEI with poor electrochemical stability. The construction of anion-derived robust interphase induced by the weakly solvated electrolyte is an effective approach for improving the cycling stability, but suffers compromised ionic conductivity due to the low dissociation degree of sodium salt (path a in Figure <a href="#anie202410494-fig-0001">1a</a>). Here, a hybrid ester-ether electrolyte is proposed to balance the trade-off via introducing the weakly solvated ether into strongly solvated ester electrolyte (path b in Figure <a href="#anie202410494-fig-0001">1a</a>). Compared with the commercial ester-based electrolyte, the designed hybrid electrolyte may possess the superiority of weak solvation with anion-reinforced structure and high ionic conductivity (Figure <a href="#anie202410494-fig-0001">1b</a>). Figure <a href="#anie202410494-fig-0001">1c</a> exhibits the ionic conductivities of commonly used ester-based electrolytes (EC: ethylene carbonate, PC: propylene carbonate), ether-based electrolytes (G1: dimethylglycol, G2: diethylene glycol dimethyl ether, THF: tetrahydrofuran) and their hybrid electrolytes with 1.0 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>. It should be emphasized that the ionic conductivity of hybrid ester-ether electrolyte is higher than that of corresponding ester and ether electrolyte, such as 11.9 mS cm<sup>−1</sup> in 1.0 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>-EC/G1 vs. 8.27 and 8.71 mS cm<sup>−1</sup> in 1.0 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>-EC and 1.0 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>-G1, respectively. While mixed ester and mixed ether electrolyte exhibit the middle ion conductivity compared with the electrolyte with a single solvent, demonstrating the entropy increase is unlikely to be the only driver of the discrepancies in conductivity (Table S1). These indicate the combination of ester and ether results in a higher diffusivity, which is expected to promote ion transport.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410494-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410494-bib-0036_R_d23707355e718" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9">9</a></sup></span>
               </p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202410494-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f3d81d41-61d5-4e1c-99ec-2dfaa7f1e39b/anie202410494-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/f3d81d41-61d5-4e1c-99ec-2dfaa7f1e39b/anie202410494-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/5ba14b6a-26ca-4fc8-ab94-15a7909b723c/anie202410494-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f3d81d41-61d5-4e1c-99ec-2dfaa7f1e39b/anie202410494-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202410494-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202410494" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Electrolyte design. (a) Schematic illustration of weakly solvated electrolyte (path a) and hybrid ester-ether electrolyte (path b). (b) Schematics of solvation structure and their merits of the three electrolytes. (c) Ionic conductivity of 1.0 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>-based electrolyte. (d) The electrostatic potential density of EC, PC, G1, G2 and THF solvents. (e) Binding energy and electron affinity energy of Na<sup>+</sup>-PC/G1, Na<sup>+</sup>-PC/G2, Na<sup>+</sup>-PC/THF, Na<sup>+</sup>-PC, and Na<sup>+</sup>-THF clusters.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>In electrolyte, the donor unit of the solvent molecule to coordinate with Na<sup>+</sup> is characterized by the negative surface electrostatic potential (ESP), which is mainly focused on the the oxygen atoms. For the strongly coordinated solvents, the absolute values of the lowest ESP (|ESP<sub>min</sub>|) and highest ESP (ESP<sub>max</sub>) are both relatively strong, such as EC and PC (Figure <a href="#anie202410494-fig-0001">1d</a>). While a significantly decreased ESP<sub>max</sub> is observed in weakly coordinated solvents with |ESP<sub>min</sub>|≫ESP<sub>max</sub>, which is consistent with the characteristics of ether solvents.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410494-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410494-bib-0041_R_d23707355e776" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Adv. Energy Mater.">10</a></sup></span> Furthermore, detailed relative permittivity (ϵ), donor number (DN), highest occupied molecular orbital (HOMO) and lowest unoccupied molecular orbital (LUMO) energy levels of ester and ether-based electrolytes are summarized in Figure S1–S2. Where ester-based electrolytes possess high ϵ, low DN and low LUMO energy levels, corresponding to the incompatibility with metal Na. Ether-based electrolytes possess low ϵ, high DN and high HOMO energy levels, which are unstable under high voltage. PC/THF system is selected according to the lowest binding energy and highest electron affinity energy with Na<sup>+</sup> in Figure <a href="#anie202410494-fig-0001">1e</a>, and the optimized volume ratio of 5 : 5 (1.0 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>-PC:THF=5 : 5, denoted as PC/THF) is applied with its highest ionic conductivity in Figure S3 and Table S2. Moreover, the Na<sup>+</sup>-PC/THF exhibits a stronger interaction than Na<sup>+</sup>-PC and Na<sup>+</sup>-THF cluster, demonstrating the superiority of hybrid PC/THF electrolyte on structural stability.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202410494-sec-0004">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202410494-sec-0004-title">2.2 Solvation Configuration</h3>
            
            <p>The solvation structure and coordination environment of hybrid ester-ether electrolyte are verified by experimental and theoretical calculations. For the pure ester-based electrolyte, 1.0 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>-PC (denoted as PC), the <sup>23</sup>Na signal is located at −10.01 ppm by nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR) in Figure <a href="#anie202410494-fig-0002">2a</a>. And this value exhibits a downshift to −8.92 ppm in PC/THF system, demonstrating the weakened electron cloud around Na<sup>+</sup>. Meanwhile, the sharper signal manifests the decreased disorder of Na<sup>+</sup> local environments in PC/THF system. <sup>19</sup>F NMR validates the increased electron density around PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anions, reflected as the upfield shifts from −72.4, −73.9 ppm to −73.1, −74.6 ppm in Figure <a href="#anie202410494-fig-0002">2b</a>, signifying the intensified Na<sup>+</sup>-PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> interactions.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410494-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410494-bib-0042_R_d23707355e828" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11">11</a></sup></span> Additionally, Fourier transform infrared (FTIR) spectra is further utilized for characterizing the microscopic change of solvation species coordinated to Na<sup>+</sup> (Figure S4). In Figure <a href="#anie202410494-fig-0002">2c</a>, there exhibits the specific vibration of PC solvents. For example, the C=O vibration peak among 1700–1850 cm<sup>−1</sup> can be divided into two peaks: free PC at ca. 1795 cm<sup>−1</sup> and coordinated PC at ca. 1755 cm<sup>−1</sup>.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410494-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410494-bib-0046_R_d23707355e843" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - ACS Energy Lett.">12</a></sup></span> The much higher wavenumbers in PC/THF system indicate the increased proportion of free PC in the inner Na<sup>+</sup> sheath, compared with the PC system. And this is also proved by the Raman spectra in Figure S5.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202410494-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/940cd841-b8c3-4e81-982f-2f748592aa6e/anie202410494-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/940cd841-b8c3-4e81-982f-2f748592aa6e/anie202410494-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/2ed37f29-88ab-4703-b23f-c9a37ef8e93c/anie202410494-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/940cd841-b8c3-4e81-982f-2f748592aa6e/anie202410494-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202410494-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202410494" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Solvation configuration. (a) <sup>23</sup>Na NMR, (b) <sup>19</sup>F NMR spectra, and (c) FTIR spectra of PC and PC/THF electrolyte. Radial distribution functions (solid lines) and coordination number (dotted lines) calculated from MD simulations in (d) PC and (e) PC/THF electrolyte. (f) Snapshots and (g) distribution of inner solvation configurations observed from MD simulations in PC and PC/THF electrolyte.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>Classical molecular dynamics (MD) simulations are performed to clarify the specific solvation configuration in PC and PC/THF electrolyte. According to the radial distribution functions and coordination number in Figure <a href="#anie202410494-fig-0002">2d</a>, <a href="#anie202410494-fig-0002">2e</a>, S6 and Table S3, PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anions extensively participate in the inner Na<sup>+</sup> solvation sheath after incorporating the weakly solvated THF solvent, with the coordination number increasing from 0.49 to 1.84. Interestingly, a much higher intensity of Na-O<sub>PC</sub> and Na-F<sub>PF6</sub> pair is observed in PC/THF system, indicating the reinforced Na<sup>+</sup>-PC and Na<sup>+</sup>-PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> interactions. The emerged Na-O<sub>THF</sub> pair at 0.232 nm confirms the partial replacement of PC by THF, with the coordination number of 0.46. As the snapshots shown in Figure <a href="#anie202410494-fig-0002">2f</a> and Figure S7, anion-reinforced solvation configuration is presented in the PC/THF system, with more PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anions and less PC solvents occupying the first Na<sup>+</sup> sheath. In addition, Figure <a href="#anie202410494-fig-0002">2g</a> and Figure S8 display the distribution of the inner solvation shell of PC and PC/THF electrolyte, where it is evident that higher species of solvent in hybrid ester-ether electrolyte endows the increased configuration diversity. Specifically, the proportion of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anion-occupied configuration is only 47 % in PC electrolyte, and this value increases up to 96 % in PC/THF electrolyte, further demonstrating the construction of anion-reinforced solvation sheath in hybrid ester-ether electrolyte.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202410494-sec-0005">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202410494-sec-0005-title">2.3 Interfacial Properties</h3>
            
            <p>To further explore the positive effect of THF cosolvent on the coordination environment, representative solvation configurations are selected for the density functional theory (DFT) calculations. The electron distribution of Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent and Na<sup>+</sup>-anion-solvent complexes are presented in Figure <a href="#anie202410494-fig-0003">3a</a>. In Na<sup>+</sup>-solvent complexes, the negative charges of solvents are mainly contributed by the O atoms in carbonyl group, while the positive moieties are concentrated on the H atoms in alkyl groups. In sharp contrast, Na<sup>+</sup>-anion-solvent configurations are negatively charged with PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anions, promising their high Coulombic interactions with the positive electrode. Molecular interactions of these solvation configurations are also probed by first-principle calculations in Figure <a href="#anie202410494-fig-0003">3b</a> and Table S4. For example, Na<sup>+</sup>(PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>)(PC)<sub>5</sub> exhibits the binding energy of −883.45 kJ mol<sup>−1</sup>, which is much weaker than −917.78 kJ mol<sup>−1</sup> in structure by alternating PC as THF, namely Na<sup>+</sup>(PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>)(PC)<sub>4</sub>(THF). Furthermore, relatively lower LUMO and higher HOMO energy levels are observed in Na<sup>+</sup>(PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>)(PC)<sub>4</sub>(THF) with −0.79 and −6.82 eV, compared with −0.76 and −7.35 eV in Na<sup>+</sup>(PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>)(PC)<sub>5</sub> (Figure <a href="#anie202410494-fig-0003">3c</a> and Table S4), indicating the reduced reduction and oxidation stability in the hybrid ester-ether based electrolyte. In Figure <a href="#anie202410494-fig-0003">3d</a>, the introduction of weakly coordinated THF solvents also exhibits superiority on enhancing the interfacial desolvation process, with a much lower desolvation energy of −217.34 kJ mol<sup>−1</sup> in Na<sup>+</sup>(PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>)(PC)<sub>4</sub>(THF) than −203.187 kJ mol<sup>−1</sup> in Na<sup>+</sup>(PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>)(PC)<sub>5</sub>.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202410494-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/95902dd0-0023-4668-bfdf-3511ead5cd07/anie202410494-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/95902dd0-0023-4668-bfdf-3511ead5cd07/anie202410494-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/54a22a74-5326-4ecf-8569-95e01cfe5cc8/anie202410494-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/95902dd0-0023-4668-bfdf-3511ead5cd07/anie202410494-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202410494-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202410494" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Interfacial properties. (a) Electrostatic potential density distribution, (b) binding energy, and (c) HOMO/LUMO energy level of representative solvation configurations. (d) Desolvation energy of Na<sup>+</sup>(PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>)(PC)<sub>5</sub> and Na<sup>+</sup>(PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup>)(PC)<sub>4</sub>(THF). (e) Nyquist plots of symmetric half-state PB||PB cells from 343 to 313 K and corresponding (f) Na<sup>+</sup> desolvation energy.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <p>Based on the Arrhenius equation, the desolvation energy (<i>E</i><sub>a</sub>) is also calculated by the temperature-dependent electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) from 343 to 313 K in Figure <a href="#anie202410494-fig-0003">3e</a> and Table S5–S6, where symmetric half-charge state PB||PB cells are assembled to eliminate the effect of electrode-electrolyte interface.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410494-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410494-bib-0047_R_d23707355e1055" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13">13</a></sup></span> In Figure <a href="#anie202410494-fig-0003">3f</a>, a much smaller desolvation energy of 5.89 kJ mol<sup>−1</sup> is observed in PC/THF electrolyte, compared with 13.23 kJ mol<sup>−1</sup> in PC electrolyte. These demonstrate the superiority of hybrid PC/THF electrolyte on decreasing the desolvation barrier with enhanced interfacial kinetics.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202410494-sec-0006">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202410494-sec-0006-title">2.4 Interfacial Chemistry</h3>
            
            <p>In-depth X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) is employed for executing the chemical components and distribution of the as-formed cathode-electrolyte interface (CEI) and solid electrolyte interface (SEI) layer. Normalized XPS fitting results of high-resolution F 1s and P 2p spectra are exhibited with different sputtering times of 0 s and 36 s, as shown in Figure <a href="#anie202410494-fig-0004">4a</a>-4h on PB and HC surface, while those for C 1s and O 1s spectra are available in Figure S9–S12. The surface of CEI formed in PC/THF system displays a strong P−F peak (687.1 eV) in F 1s spectrum and Na<sub>x</sub>PF<sub>y</sub> peak (137.7 and 136.8 eV) in P 2p spectrum.<span><sup><a href="#anie202410494-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410494-bib-0050_R_d23707355e1082" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14">14</a></sup></span> These are attributed to the accelerated reduction of PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anions in PC/THF electrolyte, which is consistent with the anion-reinforced Na<sup>+</sup> solvation sheath. The strength of P−F and Na<sub>x</sub>PF<sub>y</sub> peaks decreases with the etching time, while that of NaF peak at 684.6 eV in F 1s spectrum exhibits a slight increase at the inner CEI layer. This demonstrates the gradient inorganic structure of CEI with abundant P−F and Na<sub>x</sub>PF<sub>y</sub> species at the outer CEI and more NaF at the inner CEI.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202410494-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f2c75c52-dd86-48e2-ba53-250eed417d87/anie202410494-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/f2c75c52-dd86-48e2-ba53-250eed417d87/anie202410494-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/12542ad6-6833-43a3-9f83-c44620448c1a/anie202410494-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f2c75c52-dd86-48e2-ba53-250eed417d87/anie202410494-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202410494-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202410494" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Interfacial chemistry. In-depth XPS spectra of F 1s in (a) PC and (b) PC/THF electrolyte, P 2p in (c) PC and (d) PC/THF electrolyte on PB cathode. In-depth XPS spectra of F 1s in (e) PC and (f) PC/THF electrolyte, P 2p in (g) PC and (h) PC/THF electrolyte on HC anode. (i) Atomic ratios of CEI and SEI components in PC and PC/THF electrolyte. (j) TEM images of cycled PB and HC electrodes.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            
            <div class="paragraph-element">For the SEI formed in PC/THF system, abundant NaF is observed after etching 36 s, which is reported as the effective species for facilitating Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion and inhibiting dendrites growth with less side reactions. Moreover, large amounts of Na<sub>x</sub>PO<sub>y</sub>F<sub>z</sub> at 133.7 and 132.7 eV in P 2p spectrum are also presented in the inner SEI layer. This can be explained by the step-wise decomposition of NaPF<sub>6</sub>, as exhibited in Eq. 1–<a class="equationLink scrollableLink" href="#anie202410494-disp-0001" title="Link to equation">4</a>:<span><sup><a href="#anie202410494-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202410494-bib-0051_R_d23707355e1138" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14a - Nano Energy">14a</a></sup></span><div class="inline-equation" id="anie202410494-disp-0001"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/e6a12374-cfe7-4024-afc5-c806549556a1/anie202410494-math-0001.png"></span><math></math>
                     </span><span class="inline-equation__label">(1)</span></div>
               
               <div class="inline-equation" id="anie202410494-disp-0002"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/605b6952-549d-4a24-b505-80af20749a1c/anie202410494-math-0002.png"></span><math></math>
                     </span><span class="inline-equation__label">(2)</span></div>
               
               <div class="inline-equation" id="anie202410494-disp-0003"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/cd3fca75-04a1-4cf5-a998-9c0acee1e9a2/anie202410494-math-0003.png"></span><math></math>
                     </span><span class="inline-equation__label">(3)</span></div>
               
               <div class="inline-equation" id="anie202410494-disp-0004"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                     
                     <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/a7c98f59-a4e8-4415-92aa-482b85fee8ae/anie202410494-math-0004.png"></span><math></math>
                     </span><span class="inline-equation__label">(4)</span></div>
               </div>
            
            <p>Inorganic-rich SEI with gradient structure is constructed in the PC/THF system. Interestingly, the opposite trend of atomic ratio is observed in CEI and SEI with etching time in Figure <a href="#anie202410494-fig-0004">4i</a>, with the atomic contents of P, F and Na decreasing in PB surface and increasing in HC surface. These demonstrate more solvent-derived species in the inner CEI layer and more anion-derived species in the inner SEI layer. The thickness and uniformity of CEI and SEI are also detected by transmission electron microscopy (TEM) in Figure <a href="#anie202410494-fig-0004">4j</a>. An inhomogeneous CEI layer with the thickness range of 0 to 25.4 nm is observed, indicating the severe decomposition of PC electrolyte. The same phenomenon is also presented on HC surface with PC electrolyte. Notably, uniform interfacial layers with thinner thickness are anchored on PB and HC surface with the help of PC/THF electrolyte, suggesting its superiority on inhibiting the excessive decomposition of electrolyte. Numerous side products are accumulated on the cycled PB particles in PC electrolyte, while a relatively complete cubic structure is maintained in PC/THF electrolyte after 100 cycles in Figure S13. These findings manifest the formation of a uniform, robust inorganic-rich interfacial layer in the PC/THF system.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="anie202410494-sec-0007">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="anie202410494-sec-0007-title">2.5 Electrochemical Performance</h3>
            
            <p>To demonstrate the superiority of gradient inorganic-rich interfacial layer on both PB and HC surfaces, full cells are assembled without any preactivation treatment in Figure <a href="#anie202410494-fig-0005">5a</a>. The PB||HC cell with PC/THF electrolyte exhibits a smaller potential drop of 0.378 V with lower self-discharge of 13.33 % after resetting for 72 h, compared with 0.391 V and 21.92 % in PC electrolyte (Figure <a href="#anie202410494-fig-0005">5b</a> and Figure S14), demonstrating the improved stability in hybrid ester-ether electrolyte. As the rate capability shown in Figure <a href="#anie202410494-fig-0005">5c</a>–<a href="#anie202410494-fig-0005">5d</a>, similar capacities are realized at lower current densities of 0.2 C and 0.5 C (1.0 C=150 mA g<sup>−1</sup>), the superiority of fast interfacial kinetics in PC/THF system is displayed under high current density. Specifically speaking, high capacities of 88.6 and 65.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> are observed in PC/THF electrolyte at 3.0 C and 5.0 C with high electrode mass loading, much higher than that of 74.4 and 26.9 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> in PC electrolyte. Limited by the narrow electrochemical window of 1.0 M NaPF<sub>6</sub>-THF electrolyte (denoted as THF), obvious overcharge is presented with pretty unstable Coulumbic efficiency in Figure S15 and S16, demonstrating the poor electrochemical performance of THF electrolyte. Further analysis of rate stability is summarized in Figure <a href="#anie202410494-fig-0005">5e</a>, where PC/THF system displays the smaller overpotentials and higher capacity retention at different rates. In Figure <a href="#anie202410494-fig-0005">5f</a>–<a href="#anie202410494-fig-0005">5g</a> and Figure S17, full cells with PC/THF electrolyte deliver a stable long-term cycling at 2.0 C, with a high capacity of 64.0 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and capacity retention of 62.2 % after 300 cycles. In sharp contrast, lower values of 50.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and 46.8 % are observed in PC electrolyte. In addition, PB||HC 18650 cylindrical cell with PC/THF electrolyte maintains a desirable capacity of 524.8 mAh and capacity retention of 82.5 % after 100 cycles in Figure <a href="#anie202410494-fig-0005">5h</a> and S18, proving its potential application for large-scale production development.
</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="anie202410494-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/a70c70a1-8c96-45ac-a47d-324470b7e75f/anie202410494-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/a70c70a1-8c96-45ac-a47d-324470b7e75f/anie202410494-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/871f07aa-3ce9-4e96-9a66-244dadddefcc/anie202410494-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/a70c70a1-8c96-45ac-a47d-324470b7e75f/anie202410494-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202410494-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202410494" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                        <p>Electrochemical performance. (a) Schematic diagram of PB||HC cells. (b) Self-discharge curves of PB||HC coin cells. (c) Rate capability and corresponding (d) charge-discharge curve with PC/THF electrolyte with high electrode mass loading. (e) Overpotential and capacity retention at different current densities from rate performance. (f) Charge-discharge curves and corresponding (g) long-term cycling at 2.0 C. (h) Long-term cycling performance of PB||HC cylindrical cell.</p></div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202410494-sec-0008">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202410494-sec-0008-title">3 Conclusions</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, anion-reinforced solvation sheath and high ionic conductivity are simultaneously realized in the entropy-assisted hybrid PC/THF electrolyte. Abundant PF<sub>6</sub><sup>−</sup> anions taking participated in the inner Na<sup>+</sup> solvation sheath with enhanced desolvation process as introducing the weakly coordinated THF solvent, promoting the construction of anion-derived inorganic-rich interfacial layer. In addition, diverse weakly solvation structures induced by entropy effect in the hybrid system merit improved ionic conductivity. Taking these advantages, PB||HC full cell delivers drastically enhanced rate performance, and high capacity retention of 82.5 % after 100 cycles in industry-level PB||HC 18650 cylindrical cell. We envision that this work can spur efforts to broadly develop entropy-assisted hybrid ester-ether solutions with superior properties and to promote the practical application of sodium-ion full batteries.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
